# ☀ WAKE-UP — 2026-06-23: **THE UNION'S PLAUSIBLE ENDINGS — the grounded "what ifs" now run for the North too: a Reconstruction that finally holds, an early abolition war, forty acres enforced, a bloodless end bought with money — each one earned, each named honestly, completing the full spectrum on both sides**

**Newest (overnight run, D117):** Two nights ago the game learned the wildest ways your war could end; last night it learned the *grounded* Southern ones; tonight it finishes the set with the grounded *Northern* ones. Eight realistic ways a well-played Union war could have ended differently, laid out along the same single spectrum that runs from the plausible through the long shot to the frankly fantastical. Among them: **a Reconstruction that holds** (win the war as a war against slavery and carry the 1864 election decisively, and the 14th and 15th Amendments are enforced past 1877 instead of betrayed by the Compromise that ended Reconstruction); **an early abolition war** (free and arm the enslaved from 1861 rather than waiting for 1863); **hard war, early** (Sherman's march years sooner, the Confederacy collapsing fast); **the 1862 knockout** (drive on Richmond before the South can consolidate); **forty acres enforced** (land redistribution made real, the era's greatest unkept promise); **a compensated, bloodless end** (slavery ended by purchase, the road Lincoln actually proposed and the border states refused, sparing some 750,000 lives); **the general strike triumphant** (Du Bois's mass self-emancipation in full); and, named for the failure it would have been, **reunion without emancipation** — a Union restored with slavery left intact.

**Each one earned, each named honestly — and one named as a failure.** As on the Southern side, you open the path with a real choice and then carry your war to secure it. And the honesty is unflinching: the reunion-without-emancipation ending says plainly that a reunion preserving slavery would have been a profound moral failure, four million people left in bondage — never dressed up as a win. Every ending sits beside the sober history it departs from, with a real citation (Foner, Du Bois, McPherson) attached.

**The keystone holds — it reads your war, it never bends it.** Still a pure read-out wired to nothing in the fighting, so all nine battles play byte-for-byte as before. **The bug-hunt (five angles, eight agents) re-verified every Union history note and the books it cites, and caught one thing:** the general-strike note undercounted the Black soldiers who took up arms for the Union — "tens of thousands" where the game's own report card already says ~180,000 (about one in ten Union soldiers by war's end). Corrected to match. **Verified:** build GATE OK; the endings test 24/24 with zero page errors; the divergence and after-action tests still green; Classic still paints; no combat code touched; WCAG AA. Committed + pushed. **The full alternate-history catalog you helped design — two dozen end-states, North and South — is now live.**

**Next:** the interactive hinge-point fork cards that let you actively steer toward these endings; then wiring a reached ending to a real war-ending screen; then the codex, tutorial, and the full accessibility pass.

# ☀ WAKE-UP — 2026-06-23: **THE PLAUSIBLE ENDINGS — beneath the wild "what ifs," the game now tracks the realistic ways your Southern war could have ended differently: a recognized independence, a negotiated peace, an emancipated Confederacy, a stalemate of two Americas — each one you EARN, each set against the war that really happened**

**Newest (overnight run, D116):** Last night the game learned the boldest, most fantastical ways your war could end. Tonight it learns the *grounded* ones — the genuinely plausible alternate endings a well-played Confederate war could reach. Eight of them, laid out beneath the wild ones along a single labeled spectrum that runs from the plausible, through the long shot, to the frankly fantastical, so you always know how far from history each outcome really sits. Among them: **a recognized independence** (court Europe and win on the field until London and Paris take the Confederacy's hand — the autumn-1862 crisis that history defused); **a negotiated peace** (bleed Northern patience to the 1864 election until Washington consents to let the South go — the road the real Confederacy came closest to traveling); **an emancipated Confederacy** (Patrick Cleburne's suppressed proposal carried through — independence bought by arming and freeing the enslaved, which would have gutted the very thing the war was fought to defend); a **stalemate of two Americas**; a **Fabian survival** that simply outlasts the North's patience; a **Trans-Mississippi redoubt** west of the river; **King Cotton** finally coercing Europe; and the **Northwest seceding** to fracture the Union three ways.

**Each one earned, not clicked, and each named honestly.** As with the fantastical tier, you have to open the path with a real choice — court recognition, break Northern will, pull the Cleburne lever, survive into 1865 — *and then* carry your war to secure it; until then it reads "within reach," telling you what it would take. And the honesty holds: the emancipated-Confederacy ending says plainly that the Confederacy's founding creed was slavery, and every ending sits beside the sober history it departs from — no European power ever recognized the South, no armistice ever came, Cleburne's plan was shelved until it was far too late.

**The keystone — it reads your war, it never bends it.** Still a pure read-out: it looks at the state you've built and draws what it finds, wired to nothing in the fighting, so all nine battles play byte-for-byte as before. **The bug-hunt (five angles, eleven agents) re-verified every one of the eight new history notes and the books they cite, and caught four things** — most notably an ending (the Trans-Mississippi redoubt) that could appear merely because the calendar reached 1865, even in a war you were losing; it now requires a record you actually earned. Also fixed: a teaser that promised the Union side endings it can't yet reach, a "Fabian" ending opened by the wrong lever, and a hint that over-promised the 1864 election. All fixed and locked with tests. **Verified:** build GATE OK; the endings test 16/16 with zero page errors; the divergence and after-action tests still green; Classic still paints; no combat code touched; WCAG AA. Committed + pushed.

**Next:** the same grounded treatment for the Union side — a Reconstruction that holds, an early abolition war, forty acres enforced, a bloodless compensated end — already designed and staged; then the interactive hinge-point forks that steer toward all of it.

# ☀ WAKE-UP — 2026-06-23: **THE BOLDEST ENDINGS — play a wild card and carry your war, and the game now tracks the most outlandish ways it could end: a British war, Maximilian's legions, Lincoln living to lead Reconstruction — each one you have to EARN, each set beside what really happened**

**Newest (overnight run, D115):** You helped design this one tonight. The "Your War vs History" panel now keeps a second ledger beneath the first: **alternate endings** — the boldest, most "what if" ways your war could finish. This first batch is the *fantastical* tier, four for each side: for the South, an Anglo-American war that breaks the blockade, Maximilian's French-Mexican legions crossing the Rio Grande, Stonewall Jackson surviving Chancellorsville, and a victorious slave-power reaching for a Caribbean empire; for the North, a rapid-fire war of repeaters and Gatlings, the Tsar's fleet staying to fight, **Lincoln living to lead Reconstruction himself**, and abolition written into the Constitution years early. Each is something you **earn**, not click: you have to open the path (play the gambit, free the enslaved, bring a victory within reach) *and then* carry your war — win battles, hold the home front, win the 1864 election — to secure it. Until then it shows as "within reach," with a line telling you exactly what it would take. And every one is labeled plainly as *fantastical* and set beside the sober history it departs from — the Trent crisis that was actually defused, the Jackson who actually died, the Lincoln who was actually assassinated days after Appomattox.

**An honest line even on the wildest ending.** The Confederate "Golden Circle" — a slave empire stretching into Cuba and Central America — is in there because the ambition was real, named for what it was: the antebellum filibusters' dream of expanding slavery by conquest. It is recorded as that, never as a triumph. The whole panel keeps the rule the rest of the game keeps: it shows you how far your war has strayed; it never tilts the war to get there.

**The keystone — it reads your war, it never bends it.** The endings are pure read-out: they look at the state you've built and draw what they find, wired to nothing in the fighting. No combat code reads any of it, nothing is written back, so all nine battles play byte-for-byte as before. **The bug-hunt (five angles) re-checked every one of the eight history notes for accuracy and caught two small things** — a source citation that was rendering with a garbled ampersand, and an empty-state hint that was promising a player the *other* side's endings — both fixed and locked with a test. **Verified:** build GATE OK; the new endings test 9/9 with zero page errors; the divergence and after-action tests still green; Classic still paints; no combat code touched; WCAG AA. Committed + pushed.

**Next:** the *grounded* tier — the two dozen plausible ways a well-played war really could have ended differently (a negotiated peace, an emancipated Confederacy, a Reconstruction that finally holds) — and the interactive hinge-point forks that steer toward them.

# ☀ WAKE-UP — 2026-06-23: **THE GENERAL YOU CAN'T AFFORD TO FIRE — a political general you commission now costs extra political capital to relieve as the 1864 election nears, the bind Lincoln actually lived — and it lifts the moment the votes are counted**

**Newest (overnight run, D113):** You can already commission the war's political generals — Banks, Butler, Sigel, McClernand — for the votes and factions they bring, and watch their poor generalship drag your army. Tonight they bring a second, sharper lesson. While a political general serves, he delivers his constituency — War Democrats, German-American voters, the antislavery vote — to the war effort, so dismissing him in the run-up to the November 1864 election would cost you that support at the worst possible moment. The game now charges that bind: relieving a commissioned political general costs **extra political capital**, a surcharge that climbs year over year toward 1864 and then **vanishes the instant the election is decided** — the support is spent, and you can finally cashier him. It is exactly the trap Lincoln lived: he kept men he could not afford to fire until his re-election was safe, and only then was the worst of them let go (Butler was relieved in January 1865, just after the polls). His card now tells you so, naming the constituency and the price.

**The keystone — it changes a strategic cost, never the battle.** The surcharge is political-capital arithmetic on the President's desk; nothing in the actual fighting reads it. It is exactly zero for every general the war hands you, for the entire Confederate side, and outside the election window — so the army fights byte-for-byte as before and all nine battles are untouched. The only thing that changes is the price of dumping a Union political general you chose to commission and field, in the years before the 1864 vote.

**The bug-hunt (five angles) caught a real one.** The bind was firing on the Confederate side too — a Southern player who fielded Floyd or Pillow was told "Lincoln lived this bind," a plainly false attribution, since the Confederacy held no 1864 presidential election at all (Davis was inaugurated to a single six-year term in 1862). It's now Union-only, where it belongs; Confederate political generals keep only their ordinary cost to relieve. (Two smaller hardening fixes too — a tampered-config guard and a prose correction about how the cost rises.) **Verified:** build GATE OK; the command test 76/76 with zero page errors; Classic still paints; no combat code touched, so the battles are identical by construction; WCAG AA. Committed + pushed.

**Next:** the alternate-history *forks* — and you've just helped design the catalog: two dozen ways a well-played war can end differently, North and South, from a negotiated peace to an emancipated Confederacy to a Reconstruction that finally holds, built fantastical-tier first.

---

# ☀ WAKE-UP — 2026-06-23: **YOUR WAR, GRADED — the game now reports a card on the whole war you fought: a letter grade for the battlefield, the treasury, diplomacy, the home front, the war's purpose, and your high command — and tells you, plainly, what came after**

**Newest (overnight run, D112):** Last night the game began keeping a ledger of how your war diverged from history. Tonight it grades the war you made. A new **"After-Action"** panel on the President's Desk reports a period **report card** — an A-through-F grade, with an honest word beside each (a "C" is "Workmanlike," an "A+" is "Legendary," an "F" is "A failure") — for every part of the war you actually ran: **the battlefield** (how often you won, and at what cost in blood); **the treasury** (how far your dollar held or collapsed); **diplomacy and the blockade** (the contest over Europe's recognition); **the home front** (the people's will, and the verdict of the 1864 election); **the war's purpose**; and **your high command**. Above them sits a single overall grade. Below them, the panel reads your divergence ledger back to you, sets the men you lost beside the war's true human cost — about **750,000 dead**, North and South, by the modern demographic count that revised the old figure of 620,000 upward — and closes with a forward look at the **Reconstruction to come**, shaped by the choices you made. The same report greets you, in full, when you win the war.

**An honest grade, and an honest refusal to grade one thing.** For the Union, "the war's purpose" is graded by *when* you freed the enslaved: emancipation on history's timing earns near-full marks, freeing them early is bold (and was costly — Lincoln feared losing the border states, which is why in history he waited for Antietam), and refusing or delaying it is named plainly as a failure of will. For the Confederacy, that same panel is **not a grade at all** — the game refuses to score the Southern "cause" as a virtue, because the cause was slavery, and says so in the founders' own words (Vice-President Stephens called it the new government's "cornerstone"). It is rendered as an honest accounting and left out of the overall grade entirely. And the report never bends your war — it reads what you did; history is the par, never a rail.

**The keystone — it grades your war, it never changes it.** The whole panel is a read-out: it reads the state you built and draws a grade, and it is wired to nothing in the fighting. No part of the combat engine reads any of it, and the report writes nothing back, so all nine battles play byte-for-byte as before. The end-of-war screen was rewritten to show this report, but it keeps its old "Main Menu" button and clean-slate behavior exactly.

**The bug-hunt (five angles) found the feature sound — invariants confirmed — and caught one good thing.** The home-front grade read the nation's-will number through a guard that, by a quirk of JavaScript, would let a "not-a-number" value slip past and surface as "NaN of 100" (or quietly mislabel a graded panel as the un-graded one) in a corrupted save. It's now sanitized the same defensive way the older morale panels already are, and a new test locks it. One citation was also tightened to a more on-point source. **Verified:** build GATE OK; the new "After-Action" test 10/10 with zero page errors; Classic still paints; the strategy-desk, divergence, victory, and command tests green; no combat code touched, so the battles are identical by construction; WCAG AA. Committed + pushed.

**Next:** wiring a negotiated peace (and recognition) into an actual end-of-war so the report can greet you there too, the election-support bind for the political generals, and the deeper interactive hinge-point forks of the alternate-history deck.

---

# ☀ WAKE-UP — 2026-06-23: **YOUR WAR vs HISTORY — the game now keeps a running ledger of every way your war has diverged from the one that actually happened, and tells you the history each departure leaves behind**

**Newest (overnight run, D111):** With the whole command depth chart now in your hands, tonight the war begins to remember how *yours* differs from the real one. A new **"Your War vs History"** panel on the President's Desk keeps a living ledger of every point where your war has left the documented record — and for each one it tells you what actually happened. Refuse the Emancipation Proclamation, or issue it years early, or drag your feet and free the enslaved late; arm the enslaved as the Confederacy; keep Europe's recognition alive past the autumn of 1862; lose the 1864 election to the peace party; stand the South at the threshold of a negotiated peace; or play one of the wild cards — each is recorded with a tier (a minor thread pulled loose, a major departure, or history outright rewritten) and the real history it diverges from, plainly and even-handedly, North and South alike. A headline "divergence index" sums how far you've strayed — not a score, just a measure of distance from the past. And a new switch lets you play in **emergent-only mode**: turn off the ahistorical wild-card gambits entirely, and your war can only bend through the choices and battles of the campaign itself.

**The keystone — it shows your war, it never changes it.** The whole panel is a read-out: it reads the strategic state you've built and draws what it finds, and it is wired to nothing in the actual fighting. There is no thumb on the scale — history is the "par," never a rail; nothing here pulls your war back toward it. The proof is absolute: no part of the combat engine reads any of this, so all nine battles play byte-for-byte as they did, and a war that simply follows the historical record reads an empty ledger.

**The bug-hunt (five angles) confirmed the feature sound** — and caught a good one. The 1864-election entry didn't check which side you play: a Confederate whose home front collapsed into 1864 was being told "the 1864 election repudiates the war" with a counterfactual about *Lincoln's* re-election — nonsense for the South, which held no such election and for whom a broken Northern will is the *road to victory*, not a defeat. Now the Northern election is Union-only, and the Confederate home-front break gets its own honest entry (Davis was inaugurated to a six-year term in 1862; the South never voted the war away — it fought to military collapse). The hunt also found that a Union which freed the enslaved *late* — a real, troubling divergence — was being silently ignored; now it's recorded as a Union failing, named as plainly as any Confederate one. Three smaller fixes too (a date correction, a screen-reader toggle label, a documented note on where the history claims are checked). **Verified:** build GATE OK; the new "Your War vs History" test 14/14 with zero page errors; Classic still paints; the strategy-desk and command tests green; no combat code touched, so the battles are identical by construction; WCAG AA. Committed + pushed.

**Next:** the deeper hinge-point forks and the graded after-action report that will read this ledger back to you at war's end — or the AI "general manager" opponent that drafts its own roster against you.

---

# ☀ WAKE-UP — 2026-06-23: **STAFF YOUR DIVISIONS — under each corps you seat, you can now place the generals who lead its divisions, the cutting edge of the army; a corps is only as good as the divisions beneath it**

**Newest (overnight run, D110):** You can already appoint the general who leads your whole army and seat the generals who command its corps. Tonight you get the rung beneath that: under every corps you've staffed, you can now place the generals who lead its **divisions** — three to a corps. A division was the cutting edge of an army; Lee's hardest blows fell through A. P. Hill's "Light Division" and the divisions of Hood and Pickett, just as the Union's came through the hard-fighting divisions of Hancock's II Corps. You can only staff a division under a corps that already has a commander — the chain of command is real, so the depth chart grows into a tree you build from the top down. A division was properly a *major general's* command in **both** armies (unlike a corps, where a Union corps took a major general but a Confederate one took a lieutenant general), so a mere brigadier seated over a division leads stretched until you promote him. Seat able division commanders and the whole army fights a little better; leave them weak or vacant and it doesn't.

**The keystone — it changes the inputs, never the scoreboard.** A division commander you seat only ever feeds the army's *leadership* — one ingredient the battle already weighs — by a small, capped amount, and deliberately a *smaller* amount than a corps commander, so the chain of influence stays in proportion: the army commander matters most, then the corps staff, then the divisions. He never writes a casualty or a winner. And it's provably inert until you act: with no division staffed, the army fights byte-for-byte as it did before, and all nine historical battles are untouched. One man holds one billet — promote a division commander up to a corps and he leaves his division; vacate a corps and the divisions beneath it quietly stand down.

**An honest note on how this one was built.** This feature's *code* was written in an earlier session that got interrupted, and finished and committed by Codex — and the code is sound (it builds clean, the command test passes 69/69, and it's byte-identical to its source). But Codex stopped there: it ran no adversarial bug-hunt and wrote none of the project's decision/handoff records. So tonight's work was the close-out — running the bug-hunt that should have gated the commit, and writing the trail. **The bug-hunt (five angles) found the feature sound and caught one real thing:** the teaching text celebrated only *Confederate* divisions (Hill, Hood, Pickett) while claiming to honor both sides — the very Lost-Cause habit this project guards against — even though the ratings themselves are perfectly even-handed. Fixed by naming the Union's hard-fighting divisions (Hancock's II Corps, under Gibbon and Barlow at Gettysburg, the Wilderness, and Spotsylvania) right alongside. **Verified:** build GATE OK; the command test 69/69 with zero page errors; the committed game file is byte-identical to its source; Classic still paints; no tactical code was touched, so the nine battles are identical by construction. *(The full headless no-regression suite couldn't be re-run tonight — the 3-D library's CDN throttled this machine's test browser after the first load, the documented stall — but those tests share no code with this feature and were all green at the previous milestone.)* Committed + pushed.

**Next:** the AI "general manager" opponent that drafts and staffs its own roster against you, the election-support bind for the political generals — or a pivot to the strategic arc (alternate history, the codex, the victory-and-report screen).

---

# ☀ WAKE-UP — 2026-06-23: **COMMISSION THE POLITICAL GENERALS — you can now bring Banks, Butler, Sigel, McClernand (and the Confederacy's Floyd and Pillow) into your service, cheaply — and learn the hard way that a star on the shoulder is not the skill to use it**

**Newest (overnight run, D109):** A war is fought by a coalition, and its rifles need the men a coalition is made of. Tonight the President's Desk gets a **"Commission an officer — the political generals"** panel that lets you do exactly what Lincoln and Davis did: bring a famous politician into the army for the votes and factions he delivers, not his generalship. For a little political capital — the same clout you spend to appoint and promote — you can commission **Nathaniel Banks** (the ex-Speaker who rallied the antislavery vote), **Benjamin "Beast" Butler** (the War Democrat the Union could not afford to fire), **Franz Sigel** ("I fights mit Sigel" — worth a brigade of German enlistments), and **John McClernand** (Lincoln's fellow Illinoisan); on the Confederate side, **John Floyd** and **Gideon Pillow**, the patronage grandees who fled Fort Donelson. Each shows you a *low* combat rating beside a *high* political value, and his honest record — and once commissioned he joins the generals at your call, to appoint, promote, or seat over a corps. The lesson the war taught at a fearful price is built right into the numbers: put Banks at the head of your army and it takes the field rated **49** where Grant would field **87**. Rank and constituency are not competence.

**Honest history, both ways.** The failures are named plainly — Banks wrecked on the Red River, Butler bottled up and relieved after Fort Fisher, Sigel routed at New Market, McClernand cashiered by Grant for printing his own praises, Floyd and Pillow fleeing the surrender they helped make inevitable — and the genuine contributions are credited just as plainly: Butler's 1861 "contraband" decision that cracked Union policy open toward emancipation, Banks's free-labor and Black-regiment work in Louisiana (and the Black officers he later purged, named too). Six new figures, each with at least two real published sources behind the biography, researched and then adversarially fact-checked. One correction the checkers caught and I made: Gideon Pillow's "major general" was a Mexican-War and state-militia title — in Confederate service he was only ever a *brigadier* general, and the game now says so.

**The keystone — it changes the inputs, never the scoreboard.** Commissioning a man only adds him to your pool and spends a little capital; nothing about the actual fighting reads the list. It's provably inert until you act: with nobody commissioned, the army fights byte-for-byte as it did last night — a 132-case check against the previous build came back identical, across every general, every appointment, and every date — and all nine historical battles are untouched. A political general only ever reaches a battle the way any general does: by being appointed or seated, and then his low rating drags the army's leadership through the very same pipe a good general lifts it.

**The bug-hunt (12 agents across five angles) confirmed the feature is sound** and earned its keep, catching two real ones, both fixed: the commission *economy* could be bypassed at the code level — you could field a political general without paying for him (now gated everywhere — appoint, promote, and seat all require him to be commissioned first); and a commissioned general seated in, say, the III Corps was always labeled "I Corps" on the panel (a key-parsing slip, now correct). Three smaller hardening fixes too (a tampered-save guard, a slate-cap default, and a "no longer in service" label for a general whose war has ended). **Verified:** build GATE OK; the command test **58/58**; the 132-case byte-identity check vs the last build; all nine battle tests + the strategic-desk tests green; Classic still paints; zero page errors; WCAG AA (every colour on the new panel already passed). Committed + pushed.

**Next:** the AI "general manager" opponent that drafts and commissions its own roster against you, a division sub-tier under each corps, or the strategic arc (alternate history, the codex, the victory-and-report screen — where the political generals' election-support bind naturally lands).

# ☀ WAKE-UP — 2026-06-23: **STAFF YOUR CORPS — seat your generals at the head of each corps, and who you place (and whether his rank fits the billet) shapes how well the whole army fights**

**Newest (overnight run, D108):** For a while now you've been able to appoint and promote the *one* general who commands your whole army. Tonight you get the rest of the depth chart. On the President's Desk, a new **"Corps Command — Depth Chart"** lets you seat your generals at the head of each of the army's corps — I, II, III, IV Corps — choosing from your bench and paying a little political capital, the same clout you spend to appoint and promote. A sound corps staff lifts how well the whole army fights in the field; a weak or stretched one drags it. And rank matters, the way it really did: a Union corps was a *Major General's* command, but a Confederate corps was a *Lieutenant General's* — the South created that grade in 1862 expressly to give Longstreet and Jackson their corps. So if you seat a mere major general like Jeb Stuart over a Confederate corps, he leads stretched, below his grade — until you promote him to lieutenant general and he fills the billet properly. Seat your generals well and you'll see them take their places on the order-of-battle board, each at the head of his corps.

**The keystone — it changes the inputs, never the scoreboard.** A corps commander you seat only ever feeds the army's *leadership* — one ingredient the battle already weighs — by a small, capped amount; he never writes a casualty or a winner. And it's provably inert until you act: with no corps staffed, the army fights byte-for-byte as it did last night, across every general, every appointment, and every date of the war (a 132-case check against the previous build came back identical), and all nine historical battles are untouched. A vacant corps costs you nothing by default — staffing it is the *opportunity*, not a tax. The leadership you build is captured in the actual fight, so the order-of-battle board still forecasts the same odds — it shows your commanders, it doesn't double-count them.

**The bug-hunt (11 agents across five angles) confirmed the feature is sound** and caught three honest mistakes, all fixed: the event log congratulated a *below*-grade general for leading "above his grade" (a plain contradiction, now corrected); a seated general could be shown commanding a tiny fort "garrison" that isn't really a corps at all (now he's only named over real corps); and the teaching text dated the Confederate lieutenant-general grade to October 1862 when it was actually authorized that September (now precise). **Verified:** build GATE OK; the command test 49/49 and the order-of-battle test 16/16; the 132-case byte-identity check against the last build; all nine battle tests + the strategic-desk tests green; Classic still paints; zero page errors; WCAG AA (three colours adjusted to pass contrast on the new panel). Committed + pushed.

**Next:** the AI "general manager" opponent that drafts and staffs its own roster against you, or bringing new officers into your pool (commissions) — or the strategic arc (alternate history, the codex, the victory-and-report screen).

---

## Earlier — 2026-06-23: **SCOUT THE ENEMY — send your cavalry to reconnoiter the next battle, and how much of the enemy's army you uncover depends on how good your cavalry is**

**Newest (overnight run, D107):** The "Order of Battle" board you got last night showed your own army in full but the enemy only in outline — with a standing invitation to *scout them*. Tonight that invitation works. On the President's Desk, an **"Order a reconnaissance"** button lets you send your cavalry ahead of the next battle. It costs a little political capital — the same scarce clout you spend to appoint and promote generals — and what it buys depends on the man you've put in command and how good he is with cavalry. A reconnaissance under an ordinary commander brings back the enemy's shape: who leads them, how their corps grade out, and whether they mean to attack or stand on the defensive over what kind of ground. Put a great cavalry mind at the head of your army — a Grant, a Lee — and your troopers come back with the enemy's *whole* order of battle, corps and brigades laid bare. The intelligence is good for one battle: when you move on, you scout afresh. It's the lesson of the war's cavalry written into a button — good cavalry were an army's eyes (Stuart's rides screened Lee), and the want of them could blind a general at the worst possible moment (Stuart's absence before Gettysburg left Lee fighting half-blind).

**The keystone — it reveals the fight, it never changes it.** Scouting only ever *shows* you more of what's already there; it writes nothing into the battle. Ordering a reconnaissance touches exactly two things — it spends your capital and records what your cavalry saw — and no part of the actual fighting ever reads that record, so a campaign where you never scout plays byte-for-byte identical to last night's build, and all nine historical battles are untouched. The reveal is keyed to the specific next engagement, so stale intelligence from a battle you've already moved past quietly falls away.

**The bug-hunt (9 agents across five angles) returned "safe to commit"** and earned its keep on two small but real points. It caught that the scout's capital was being checked one way and spent another (a rounding mismatch the promotion feature had already been hardened against — now fixed the same way), and — the more interesting one — that the posture report ("the enemy means to attack") was reading the *historical* attacker and ignoring the game's alt-history option to swap who attacks, so after you reverse the roles of a battle the advisory could have described the wrong side; it now honors the swap exactly as the battle resolver does, so the scouting stays truthful even when you rewrite history. **Verified:** build GATE OK; the order-of-battle test 15/15 and the command test 40/40; all nine battle tests + the strategic-desk tests green; Classic still paints; zero page errors; WCAG AA (one colour darkened to pass contrast). Committed + pushed. *(This one was built across a mid-run pause — you asked me to stop using the internet partway through vetting; I held everything uncommitted until you said resume, then finished the gate cleanly.)*

**Next:** the general-manager *moves* — seating your generals into corps and division commands over this same order of battle (promote / relieve / reassign) — or the AI opponent that drafts and scouts against you.

## Earlier — 2026-06-22: **THE ORDER OF BATTLE — you can now see the army you and your foe will field in the next engagement, down to the corps and brigades**

**Newest (overnight run, D106):** A new **"Order of Battle"** panel on the President's Desk shows you the army you'll take into your next battle — your appointed general at its head, then its corps, then the brigades under each, every one with its rating and strength. For the historical battles it's the real order of battle, with the real commanders (Porter's brigade, Doubleday's division, the Rock of Chickamauga); for the many engagements the game generates, it's a faithful estimate built from your army's real strength and the year — its corps and brigades laid out and rated, but labeled by formation, never with an invented officer's name. The enemy's array is shown only in outline — their overall strength, roughly how many brigades, and who commands — with a standing invitation to **scout them**: the detail is what reconnaissance will buy. This is the long-promised "roster screen," and it's the shared piece of plumbing two bigger features have been waiting on: the AI "general manager" opponent that drafts and shuffles its own roster, and the pre-battle scouting that peels back that enemy outline. Both are now buildable on top of it.

**The keystone — it shows the fight, it never changes it.** The whole panel is a read-out: it reads the strategic state and the rating system and draws what it finds, and it is wired to nothing in the actual battle. The proof is absolute — the generated game file is byte-for-byte the previous build *plus* the new module and a single line that draws the panel, with not one existing line changed, so every one of the nine battles plays identically. The build's no-fudge wall now also watches the new module, so a future edit can't quietly make it cheat.

**The bug-hunt (15 agents across five angles) returned "not safe as-is" — and was right.** It caught a genuinely important one: for a multi-day battle the panel shows the *opening* day's forces (which is correct — that's the fight you walk into), but it was labeling them under the whole battle's name with no qualifier, so it could announce a Confederate edge under the bare word "Gettysburg" — a battle the Union won. That's the exact anti-Lost-Cause trap an earlier feature had already had to fix, resurfacing at the display layer; it now says plainly "the opening engagement." It also caught a build-gate change I *thought* I'd made but a failed edit had silently dropped (now actually applied), a quirk where the generated artillery was quietly deleting ~13% of an army's men and could flip which side looked larger (now the men are conserved exactly), and a spot where a half-sourced strength figure was being labeled "verified" (now honestly marked as an estimate). All five findings fixed and re-checked. **Verified:** build GATE OK (no-fudge ✓ now covering the new module); the new order-of-battle test 14/14; byte-identity 0 changed lines vs the last commit; all nine battle tests + the strategic-desk tests green; Classic still paints; zero page errors; WCAG AA. Committed + pushed.

**Next:** the AI general-manager opponent and the depth-chart moves (promote/transfer/commission over this roster), or the pre-battle scouting that reveals the enemy's order of battle — both now unblocked.

## Earlier — 2026-06-22: **RATING SYSTEM LIVE DEV-TRAITS — your generals now grow (or stall, or fall) over the war, each on his own historical arc**

**Newest (overnight run, D105):** The rating system already let a general's standing rise with victory and fall with defeat. Tonight it gets the missing Madden piece: a hidden **development trait** for every named general that shapes *how* he grows. Grant is a "Rising Star" who climbs from obscurity to the summit and whom a near-disaster like Shiloh's first day cannot sink; McClellan is a "Plateau" — a brilliant organizer with a low ceiling whose wins never become the killing blow; Burnside and Hooker are "Overpromoted," raised past their level and apt to a hard fall; Lee and Jackson are "Established Legends" already at the peak, holding their names through the bloodiest fields; Bragg is "Quarrelsome," his gains undone by feud; Hood is a "Volatile Aggressor" whose costly assaults can crater his army and his name. A general's overall rating now drifts up or down across the campaign along the arc his trait allows — and a new **"Career Arc"** panel on the President's Desk shows it: his trait, the line his rating has traced from where he started to where he is now to his peak, and a verdict — rising, holding, or fading.

**The honest, anti-Lost-Cause thesis baked into the numbers.** The Union's best generals are the *risers* — Grant, Sherman, Sheridan, Thomas develop to the very top, rated as high as Lee and Jackson — which is the real history the Lost Cause denies: the North did not win by brute numbers alone, it *found and elevated* the better commanders while the Confederate command calcified (Bragg's feuds) and destroyed itself (Hood's Tennessee campaign). Command failures are named just as plainly on the Union side (McClellan's stall, Burnside's and Hooker's over-promotion) as on the Confederate. Every assessment is marked "estimated" — a calibrated reading of a documented career, each with real sources — and nothing is laundered (Forrest's Klan leadership and the Lost Cause's slander of Longstreet are named, not hidden).

**The keystone — it changes the inputs, never the scoreboard.** A dev-trait only shapes a general's *reputation*, which the army already reads as one ingredient of how well it's led; it never writes a casualty or a winner. And it is provably inert when off: a general with no assigned trait evolves byte-for-byte as he did last night, across every kind of battle outcome and at both the floor and the ceiling. Because the whole change lives in the strategic command layer, the nine tactical battles are untouched and identical by construction.

**The bug-hunt (29 agents across five angles) returned "safe to commit"** and earned its keep: it caught a genuinely embarrassing one — a citation that welded the wrong book under George Thomas's name (a title that's actually Eliot Cohen's, not Larry Daniel's), now corrected — and a Burnside note that softened Fredericksburg into him being "proved right," now rewritten to name the futile assaults on Marye's Heights plainly. It also hardened the feature against a corrupted save (a tampered development record can no longer poison a rating or print "NaN"), and made the build's no-fudge wall structurally cover the new code. All eleven findings fixed. **Verified:** build GATE OK; the command test 34/34; the rating/desk/camp/officers tests green; Classic still paints; zero page errors; WCAG AA. Committed + pushed.

**Next:** the order-of-battle bridge that connects the strategic next-battle to a tactical order of battle — the shared plumbing the AI "general manager" opponent and the pre-battle scouting both need.

---

## Earlier — 2026-06-22: **RATING SYSTEM R-6 — the badges are now ON in the real battles: the rating system finally changes how the historical fights play**

**Newest (overnight run, D104):** For five nights the rating system has been a quiet read-out — it could grade a brigade, draw its OVR, even surge a Madden-style X-Factor, but it never actually touched the nine historical battles (those were proven byte-for-byte identical whether the system was on or off). Tonight it goes live: **43 documented commander-and-unit traits are handed to the brigades that earned them, across all nine battles.** Jackson's brigade stands like a stone wall at Bull Run; McClellan's army carries "The Slows" into the delayed, hesitant assault at Antietam's bridge; Burnside's doomed waves at Fredericksburg carry "Rigid Plan"; Bragg feeds his men in "Piecemeal" at Shiloh and Chickamauga; the 20th Maine fixes bayonets out of ammunition at Little Round Top; Thomas becomes the Rock of Chickamauga. The flaws are named as plainly as the virtues, and on **both** sides — the Union's command failures are rated as honestly as the Confederacy's brilliance (anti-Lost-Cause). You can now see a brigade's traits as accessible chips in the battle HUD.

**The keystone — these are inputs, never an output gate.** A badge only nudges the soldiers' levers (how steadily they rally, how fast they march, how well they fire); the universal combat model still decides the casualties and the winner. The proof: a deterministic sweep across the sandbox and all nine battles shows the badges shift the *bleeding* but essentially never the *verdict* — every multi-day battle's per-phase winner is byte-for-byte identical with the badges off, and the one single-battle flip that does occur is *toward* history (Chancellorsville tips to the Confederates — Jackson's flank march, Lee's masterpiece). And with the badges switched off, all ten fights are still byte-for-byte identical to last night's build. Every one of the nine battle tests still lands on its historical result with the badges biting: Pickett's Charge is still repulsed, Burnside still breaks on the stone wall, the Rock of Chickamauga still holds.

**An honest Shiloh call.** Shiloh in the engine is a single continuous fight (it can't model "nearly lost on day one, won on day two"), and it's already a coin-flip. Penalizing the surprised, green Union defenders flipped it to a Confederate win — against history — so I did NOT do that: the Union being caught napping is carried by the scenario's surprise attack and its teaching cards, while the thing that actually saved the army — the desperate Hornet's Nest stand by Prentiss and W.H.L. Wallace (who died holding the line) — is what gets modeled. That nudges the Union back to winning, the right way.

**The bug-hunt (16 agents) returned "safe to commit"** and caught a good one: the catalog advertised a "doomed double-edge" downside for the grand-charge ability that the engine never actually implemented — so the note now says plainly that it ships as upside-only (and it's only placed on a charge that historically succeeded). A few smaller honesty fixes to the records and one latent code cleanup, all applied. **Verified:** build GATE OK; byte-identity 30/30 vs last commit; all nine battle tests green with badges on; the rating/preset/arms/logistics tests green; WCAG clean; Classic still paints; zero page errors. *(This was fought through a memory-starved machine — the headless-browser tests kept crashing for lack of RAM and had to be retried until a window opened.)* Committed + pushed.

**Next:** the "general manager" follow-up (an AI opponent that drafts its own roster, trading generals between theaters, the full depth chart) once its plumbing lands — or the deferred scouting — then living reputation that raises a general's rating over the war.

---

## Earlier — 2026-06-22: **RATING SYSTEM R-6 — every "Verified" fact in the game now carries two real sources (and the build enforces it forever)**

**Newest (overnight run, D103):** A quieter milestone, but a load-bearing one for a teaching game that means its anti-Lost-Cause promise. The game stamps a great many of its facts "Verified" — weapon specs, artillery doctrine, engineering feats, the officers' signature traits, cabinet politics. The standing rule is that "Verified" means *at least two independent published sources*. An audit found the game was quietly breaking its own rule in **47 places across six data files** — claims marked Verified that rested on one source or none. So I ran a citation sweep: fifteen research agents, each finding and then a second adversarially *double-checking* real published sources (web-verified, with a hard rule to *downgrade* a claim to "estimated" rather than ever invent a citation). Every one of the 47 now carries two or more real, checked sources — and a new gate in the build will now **fail the build outright** if anyone ever again stamps a claim "Verified" without two sources behind it. The rule is no longer a promise; it's enforced by the machine.

**Honest history cuts both ways.** Two calls worth noting: the "beloved general" trait stays marked *estimated* rather than lean on Douglas Southall Freeman's famous-but-Lost-Cause-tinged Lee biography; and Forrest's "feared" reputation now cites two modern critical biographies instead of a worshipful 1899 one. A few small factual slips surfaced and were fixed too — most notably a vivid quote about the wall at Fredericksburg that had been wrongly credited to the National Park Service (it actually traces to a fan site), now paraphrased honestly. None of this touches a single battle: every fight plays byte-for-byte identical to last night's build, because only the *citations and labels* changed, never a weapon's range or a unit's strength.

**The bug-hunt was thorough (34 agents) and the verdict was "safe to commit."** It re-checked every new citation (all real, none fabricated) and tried hard to break the new gate — finding that a stray capital letter or trailing space on the word "Verified" could once have slipped past it. I closed that hole (the gate now reads the stamp forgivingly) and verified the fix. The rest of its findings were minor hardening notes with no live impact, logged for later.

**Verified:** the build passes with its new `citations ✓` stage; the gate was confirmed to *catch* all 47 violations before the fix and pass after; all ten battles byte-for-byte identical to the last commit; the ratings/engineering/cabinet/weapons/cannon/arms/decisions probes green; Classic still paints; zero page errors. Committed + pushed.

**Next:** now that the catalog is citation-clean, the rest of R-6 can hand those badges to the real 1862–63 brigades (which *will* change how battles play, so it needs a balance pass) and switch on living reputation; or the GM "arms race" features once their plumbing lands.

---

## Earlier — 2026-06-22: **RATING SYSTEM Q9 — the GM promotion economy: promote your generals**

**Newest (overnight run, D102):** You can now *promote* your generals. On the President's Desk, a new **"Officer Corps — Promotions"** panel lets you raise a general one grade up the ladder — Brigadier, Major General, Lieutenant General, full General — and it costs you a **two-part budget**: your **political capital** (the same scarce clout you spend to relieve a popular general) *and* a new **seniority** pool that grows slowly over the war. The catch is the honest one: a promotion **earned** on a strong record lifts the man's confidence and standing, but promoting a favorite **past his merit** costs extra *and* damages his reputation — and because reputation is half of his overall rating, **over-promoting a man can actually make him a *worse* commander on paper**. Rank, the game quietly teaches, is not the same as ability (the real politics Lincoln lived). Promote a deserving general and the army he leads genuinely takes the field a touch sharper — but only ever through his improved competence feeding the battle's inputs, never by editing the result. And a campaign in which you never promote anyone plays byte-for-byte identical to last night's build.

**The arms race comes later — by design.** The full "general manager" still has pieces to come: an AI opponent that drafts and promotes its own roster against you, trading generals between theaters, and the deeper Army→Corps→Division depth chart. Each of those needs a piece of plumbing that doesn't exist yet (an enemy-command model wired to combat without letting a rating *force* an outcome; theater data; a strategy-to-battle order-of-battle bridge — the same bridge the deferred "scouting" feature needs), so they're logged for the next GM increment rather than half-built. This milestone ships the player-side promotion economy, complete and clean.

**The bug-hunt earned its keep — pre-emptively, again.** Seven agents across four angles found two issues plus one from the completeness critic — all low-severity and *latent* (none bites today). I fixed all three anyway, because this is the foundation the depth-chart features build on: a rounding mismatch between the cost check and the charge (harmless while all costs are whole numbers); an unrecognized-rank fallback that would have over-graded a future non-named officer two grades too high; and a tampered-save guard that now re-clamps an out-of-bounds seniority balance the moment a save loads. The critic's verdict: sound, no-fudge wall intact, byte-identical, may commit.

**Verified:** GATE OK (no-fudge ✓); the command probe 25/25 (nine new promotion checks); byte-identity across the sandbox + all 9 battles vs last commit IDENTICAL, with a live check that a promotion really stores the grade and lifts the general's leadership; the ratings/desk/camp/war-department probes green; Classic still paints; zero page errors; the WCAG auditor passed (it darkened one new warning colour to AA). Committed + pushed.

**Next: the GM follow-up** — the AI-GM, transfers, commissions, and the full depth-chart tree — once their plumbing lands; or the deferred scouting; then the negative-badge + provenance sweep.

---

## Earlier — 2026-06-22: **RATING SYSTEM R-5 — the prosopography scale-out: inspect your army down to the men**

**Newest (overnight run, D101):** The Madden layer now has *people in it*. This is the data substrate the whole "Soldier's Story" pillar was built for. Select a brigade on the battlefield and you now see a **MEN OVR** — the average rating of the men in the ranks — beside the brigade's combat rating, with a little hatched bar that marks it as a *derived* (estimated) number rather than a *sourced* one. Under the hood, the engine can now take any brigade and build a small "muster roll" of representative soldiers — a captain, a sergeant, a private — each a real rated person with a name, and any one of them can be *promoted* up the ranks (the play-as-anyone hook: a private's latent command ability surfaces as he climbs). The crucial trick — the thing that lets a war of hundreds of thousands of men run on an 8 GB laptop — is **lazy materialization**: a brigade carries just *one* average number, and individual soldier records are built only when you actually look at one. A 100,000-man brigade still only ever builds a handful of rows on demand, never one per man. And because nothing here touches the fight, every battle plays byte-for-byte identical to last night's build.

**Generated soldiers vs documented ones — honestly distinguished.** A real, sourced figure (Jackson) shows a *solid* provenance bar; a generated stand-in shows a *hatched* one and a "~ derived" tag — so the player never mistakes an estimate for a citation. It's CVD-safe: the difference rides the hatch pattern, a glyph, and a word, never colour alone. Generated names are period-appropriate and deterministic (the same brigade always yields the same muster), with real muster-roll names a later content pass.

**The bug-hunt earned its keep — pre-emptively.** Eleven agents across four angles found four issues, all low-severity and all *latent* (none bites today — they'd only bite later, once a soldier's stats start changing over a career). I fixed all four anyway, because R-5 is the foundation the next features build on: the materialized people were sharing their underlying data *by reference* with the master ratings file, so a future "this soldier gained experience" tick could have silently corrupted the canonical data. Now every materialized person gets its own private copy, and the promotion function guards against a bad rank input and always returns a fresh object. Nothing the future builds on this can quietly rewrite the source of truth.

**Verified:** GATE OK (no-fudge ✓); the ratings probe 19/19 (incl. the lazy-materialization invariant and the four hardening guards); byte-identity across the sandbox + all 9 battles vs last commit IDENTICAL; Classic still paints; the field/officers/command/desk/camp probes green; zero page errors; the WCAG auditor passed (it lifted five slightly-too-dim labels to AA). Committed + pushed.

**Next: the GM depth-chart *moves*** — now unblocked by this per-person spine: promoting, transferring, and commissioning generals for political capital, with an AI opponent that drafts its own roster too. Then the negative-badge + provenance sweep.

---

## Earlier — 2026-06-22: **RATING SYSTEM Q8 — the camp loop: drill your army between battles**

**Newest (overnight run, D100):** You can now *grow* your army between battles. A new **"Camp" tab** on the President's Desk lets you drill the men in four ways — musketry, maneuver, entrenching, endurance — each raising the army's training toward a ceiling but costing fatigue (tired troops drill poorly, so rest matters), or you can **delegate** the drilling to your field officer for a steady hands-off gain. A well-drilled army takes the field measurably sharper (better fire, steadier morale, surer supply, fresher legs), and victory **seasons** it further while a battle that bleeds it white **dulls its edge** as green replacements come in. The tab also shows your sitting general's overall rating climbing with his record over the war. It's all a *read-out that seeds the inputs* — the drilled army conditions stronger, but nothing is ever written to the scoreboard, and a campaign that never opens the Camp plays byte-for-byte as before (a one-flag "engaged" gate keeps the whole loop inert until you use it).

**The bug-hunt caught a real one.** A 21-agent hunt found that the "this battle bled us white" check was comparing a raw casualty count against a 0–100 strength *index* — wrong units — so nearly every real battle read as "bloody," which silently meant a victory **never** seasoned the army (the headline feature was inverted to decay-only). Fixed to compare the casualty *share* (your losses vs the total), re-tuned, and the test re-tooled with realistic numbers plus a pyrrhic-victory case (a battle you won but bled heavily in correctly still dulls the army). Three smaller cleanups too.

**Verified:** GATE OK; the camp probe 8/8; byte-identity across sandbox + all 9 battles vs the last commit IDENTICAL; the strategic conditioning probes unchanged (an un-camped army conditions exactly as before); Classic still paints; zero page errors; WCAG clean. Committed + pushed.

**A process note (new standing rule, Aaron):** at ~25% remaining context on a long/looping run, stop at the next clean milestone and hand off a paste-ready continuation prompt rather than grind a strained window — now enforced in memory + the operating docs. This milestone was built, then closed out cleanly across that handoff.

**Next: R-5 — the prosopography scale-out** (bulk per-person rating rows + lazy materialization + the play-as-anyone hook), then the GM depth-chart moves, then the negative-badge + provenance sweep.

---

## Earlier — 2026-06-22: **RATING SYSTEM Q7 — the GM matchup layer: who to appoint, and the pre-battle scouting board**

**Newest (overnight run, D99):** The rating system now pays off where you make decisions. On the President's Desk, each general now shows not just one overall number but an **Attack rating and a Defend rating** — the situational truth beside "general competence" (the audacious Jackson grades higher on the attack, the cautious McClellan higher on the defensive) — surfaced on his card and on every name in the appoint pool, so you can match the man to the job. And before any battle, the side-choice screen now shows an **"Order of Battle — the Matchup"** board: each army's **force rating** (its brigades' quality, men-weighted) and total strength, the principal commander, and a labeled **predicted-edge bar** — a Madden-style scouting read-out of the odds, drawn straight from the historical order of battle. It only ever *forecasts*; it never touches the result (the whole thing is a pure read-out — every one of the nine battles plus the sandbox is byte-for-byte identical to last night's build).

**The bug-hunt earned its keep — and protected the history.** A 21-agent adversarial hunt caught a serious one (rated HIGH by the completeness critic): the multi-phase battles store their order of battle as separate per-day/per-sector slices, and the first version *summed all of them* as if they were one army — which made the board forecast a **Confederate edge at Gettysburg**, a battle the larger Union army won. On a teaching tool built to refute the Lost Cause, that's exactly the falsehood we must not print. The fix scopes a multi-phase battle's board to its **opening engagement** and labels it as such — so Gettysburg's board now reads as **day 1** (when the Confederates genuinely did drive the Union back through the town), not a verdict on the battle the Union won. The hunt also restored the commander line on the marquee battles (their leaders live per-phase), hardened a dedup edge case, and corrected a comment. A sanity scan of all nine forecasts confirmed the rest read true — Fredericksburg shows the Union with a clear force edge, exactly the point: they had the numbers and still broke against the stone wall.

**Verified:** build GATE OK (no-fudge ✓); ratings probe 18/18 (incl. an explicit anti-Lost-Cause framing guard); byte-identity across sandbox + all 9 battles vs last commit IDENTICAL; the Command-desk and side-choice probes green; Classic still paints (`nonBlank:346`); zero page errors; the WCAG auditor passed all three new surfaces (zero failures). Committed + pushed.

**A scope note:** this milestone ships the GM layer's *read-out* — the matchup board and the dual rating. The full "general manager" *moves* (promote, transfer, trade generals between theaters, the AI opponent drafting its own roster) need per-billet data on corps and division commanders that doesn't exist yet — that's the prosopography pass (R-5) — so those moves come after it. Next up is **Q8, the between-battle camp loop**: drilling your army to raise its ratings, scouting the enemy before a fight, and watching a general's rating rise with victory over the war.

## Earlier — 2026-06-22: **RATING SYSTEM R-4 — the X-Factors: the Madden surge that visibly swings a fight (and provably can't cheat)**

**Newest (overnight run, D98):** This is the dramatic one. A brigade with an X-Factor badge can now go "in the zone" — Jackson's *Foot Cavalry* on the march, Thomas as the *Rock of Chickamauga* when the line is breaking, Chamberlain's *Bayonet!* when the cartridges run out — and surge: a glowing marker, a "⚡" announcement, and a real swing in the fight. The proof it swings *honestly*: I tagged one Confederate brigade with Foot Cavalry in a deterministic sandbox and it **flipped a Union win into a Confederate win** — and yet the total casualties moved only ~17%, and the command bonus the surge can grant peaked at 0.47, well under the hard 0.9 ceiling. The rating reaches the result the only way it's ever allowed to: through the soldiers' bounded inputs (they march faster, they hold longer), never by writing the casualty count or the winner. Every X-Factor surges exactly one channel — a speed badge speeds the march and nothing else; a command badge nudges the aura toward the wall and no further — and the whole thing is realism-scaled (dramatic at Arcade, tight at Historian). No battle changes at all today, because no scenario has been handed an X-Factor yet (that's a later pass) — all nine battles plus the sandbox are byte-for-byte identical to last night's build, proven by a direct cross-build diff.

**How it was verified (and an honest note on the environment).** The standard probe suite kept stalling intermittently tonight on the same Three.js-CDN page-load issue from the R-3 night — so, as before, the authoritative check was a harness that runs all nine battles plus the sandbox deterministically on the new build *and* on last night's committed build and diffs them: **all ten identical**, zero page errors. The ratings probe (17/17) adds the X-Factor logic checks and the no-fudge replay gate; the visual probe captured all ten 2D/3D scenes cleanly; Classic still paints. Then an adversarial bug-hunt — 23 agents, six angles, each finding independently refuted — surfaced seven issues, none severe and all *latent* (they'd only bite later, when badges get assigned to real units). I fixed all seven anyway, so the foundation is clean before the next milestone builds on it: the marquee one was a speed badge that could quietly double-count (once as a baseline, once as the surge), now clamped to a single bounded band; plus the lightning glyph is now drawn as a vector so it can't turn into a "tofu box" on some systems.

**Verified:** build GATE OK (no-fudge ✓); ratings probe 17/17; byte-identity across sandbox + all 9 battles (new build vs last commit) IDENTICAL; tactical visuals 10/10; Classic `nonBlank:346`; 0 page errors. Committed + pushed. **For next session:** re-run the standard `load` probe suite once the CDN is healthy (serve threaded; if it still stalls, switch the runner to wait for DOM-ready or vendor Three.js — a small worthwhile fix).

**Next: Q7 — the GM franchise layer**, the "be the general manager" pass: a depth-chart roster (Army → Corps → Division → Brigade, each billet showing its officer's OVR/grade/badges), roster moves that cost political capital, the dual Attack/Defend rating made visible, and a pre-battle matchup screen — all read-outs and input-seeding over the rating spine the last four nights built, no new combat path.

## Earlier — 2026-06-22: **RATING SYSTEM R-3 — the badge engine: a rating now touches combat (inside the no-fudge wall)**

**Newest (overnight run, D97):** This is the first increment where a rating actually *moves the fight*. A guarded one-token factor (`fldBadgeFactor`) multiplies into the engine's existing fire / rally / melee / march lines: a brigade with a fire badge shoots a little harder, "green levies" waver a little sooner, McClellan's "Slows" drags his march. Crucially it's all **inputs, never the scoreboard** — and the no-fudge wall is now *enforced by the build itself*: a new build-gate scans the rating module and FAILS the build if any badge code ever tries to write a casualty, a winner, or unit strength directly (I verified it fires on planted `u.men=0`, `u["men"]=0`, and `sev["attrition"]=2` writes, dot- and bracket-notation). Every effect is bounded by a realism-scaled cap (Arcade generous, Historian tight), so "stack every positive badge" can't saturate a lever, and negatives only ever *damp* their own unit, never buff the enemy. Badges aren't assigned to any shipped battle yet (that's the later sweep), so **every one of the nine battles is byte-for-byte identical** — proven, not assumed.

**An honest note on how it was verified.** Tonight the standard probe suite couldn't run: headless Chrome's page-load event stopped firing for the game page (the Three.js CDN script stalls in headless — it broke even the last committed build identically, while a blank page loads in 73ms and the "DOM-ready" event loads everything including Three.js fine). Two causes: the little single-threaded test server chokes on the page's ~170 image/asset requests, and the CDN load-wait stalls. So rather than push unvetted, I verified R-3 a *stronger* way — a harness that runs all nine battles plus the sandbox deterministically on the new build and on the last committed build and diffs them: **all ten identical**, zero page errors, plus the full badge-logic checks (cap clamps to exactly +0.10 at Balanced, scales to +0.16 Arcade / +0.06 Historian, negatives land at 0.92). An adversarial bug-hunt then caught two things, both fixed: the build-gate missed bracket-notation writes (now closed), and a probe referenced a badge key that doesn't exist.

**Verified:** build GATE OK (now with a `no-fudge ✓` stage); byte-identity across sandbox + all 9 battles (new build vs last commit) IDENTICAL; badge logic correct; 0 page errors. Committed + pushed. **For the next session:** re-run the standard `load` probe suite once the environment is healthy — serve with a *threaded* http server, and if `load` still stalls on the CDN, switch the probe runner to wait for DOM-ready (the documented offline fix) or vendor Three.js locally.

**Next: R-4 — the X-Factors**, the dramatic part: an "in the zone" surge (Foot Cavalry, the Rock of Chickamauga, Bayonet!, Earned in Blood) that glows + announces + visibly swings a fight — while still routed through the capped command-bonus path so it can never breach the no-fudge wall.

## Earlier — 2026-06-22: **RATING SYSTEM R-2 — the OVR read-out is on screen (A–F report-card grades)**

**Newest (overnight run, D96):** The Madden layer is now *visible*. R-1 wired the persona into the numbers; **R-2 puts those numbers on screen, graded A–F**, with zero change to the simulation (a pure read-out — every battle stays byte-for-byte identical). On the President's Desk, each general's card now leads with his **OVR** and an **A+/A/B/C/D/F report-card grade** (Grant reads A+ "Legendary," Bragg an F, McClellan a C — exactly his "great organizer, poor fighter" profile), and the appoint-pool lists every general with his grade and OVR so you can compare at a glance. On the battlefield, selecting a brigade now shows that unit's **computed OVR and grade** — its men's experience, its weapon, its condition, and the colonel's command aura, blended into one Madden-style number that *rises when a general rides up and falls when he dies* (it's computed live, never stored).

Everything is **triple-encoded for accessibility**: the meaning rides the number, the letter grade, and the word — all in high-contrast text — with colour used only as a decorative accent stripe, so a colour-blind player loses nothing (the green→amber→red band ramp is never the sole cue). The WCAG auditor passed all three new surfaces; and the bug-hunt earned its keep — it caught that the auditor's own contrast fix had been computed against pure black instead of the actual (lighter) desk gradient and still fell short of the AA bar, so two long-standing sub-AA section labels are now genuinely AA (measured against the real `.sheet` ground). The hunt also caught an audit comment that had leaked into the rendered page and a half-applied HTML-escape, both fixed.

**Verified:** build GATE OK; the ratings probe 14/14 and the command probe 16/16 (new UI assertions); the full no-regression suite green — all 28 probes, every battle byte-identical; Classic still paints (`nonBlank:346`); zero page errors. Adversarial bug-hunt (three Opus lenses, refute-verified, plus a completeness critic): two confirmed findings + two critic findings, all fixed and re-vetted. Committed + pushed.

**Next: R-3 — the badge engine**, the first place a rating actually *touches* combat: a guarded one-token factor that's identity (no-op) until a unit carries badges, plus the global per-lever stacking cap and a build-gate assertion that no badge may ever write a casualty or a winner directly (the no-fudge wall, enforced not just promised). Ships the first Star/Superstar badges and the Verified named flaws (Bragg's "Piecemeal," McClellan's "Slows," Burnside's "Rigid Plan," green levies, powder-shy).

## Earlier — 2026-06-22: **RATING SYSTEM R-1 — officer derivation wired in; the persona is now the source of truth**

**Newest (overnight run, D95):** The Madden layer started biting. R-0 (the night before) shipped the rating *data spine* as pure functions touching nothing. **R-1 wires the documented persona into the two pipes the game already reads** — a general's command rating on the President's Desk, and a field officer's command aura on the battlefield — so a leader's history now *drives* his numbers. The hard rule held: the persona only seeds the *inputs* (a general's skill, an officer's quality/range/durability) at the moment an army or a battle is built; nothing is ever read when a casualty or a winner is decided (the no-fudge wall). And anyone not yet rated is *byte-for-byte identical* to before.

I authored **nine strategic commanders** — Lee, Jackson, Albert Sidney Johnston, Bragg, Longstreet for the South; McClellan, Grant, Burnside, Meade for the North (the principal generals of the nine history-hardened battles) — each as a 15-attribute persona, with the documented flaws named plainly (McClellan's "the slows," Burnside's "rigid plan," Bragg's "piecemeal commitment") and real sources cited. Each persona is *calibrated* so the skill it derives reproduces the existing, playtested skill number **exactly** — so the whole strategic layer (army strength, auto-resolve, the appoint-pool grades) is unchanged on the numbers, but the *source* of those numbers is now the man's history, ready for the next increment to show his full OVR, grade, and badges.

On the battlefield, the seven Bull Run commanders now take their command quality and durability from their personas (their auras shift by a hair, ≤0.005 — the calibration tolerance). The one real bug the work surfaced: leader ids are *reused across battles* (`ld_jackson` is the 1861 brigadier at Bull Run but a corps commander at Antietam and Chancellorsville), so I made the link an **explicit opt-in** — a leader derives from a persona only when it names one — which is collision-proof and keeps every other battle identical. (Chancellorsville's probe passing unchanged proves it.)

**Verified:** build GATE OK; the ratings probe 13/13 and the command probe 15/15 (both with new R-1 guards, including a cross-scenario collision guard and a single-source-of-truth guard); the FULL no-regression suite green — all nine battles byte-identical, plus the whole systems suite; Classic still paints (`nonBlank:346`); zero page errors. An adversarial bug-hunt (five Opus lenses, each finding refute-verified, plus a completeness critic) confirmed **no real defects** in the finders and surfaced **one** low-severity polish item — that two more reads of a general's skill should also flow through the new derivation — which I fixed (byte-identical today, drift-proof tomorrow). Committed + pushed.

**Next: R-2** — the OVR read-out UI: a general's overall rating, leadership word, and **A–F report-card grade** on the Command desk and a unit-inspect line, triple-encoded for accessibility (number + word + shape, never color alone). Pure display over the numbers the persona already feeds.

---

## Earlier — 2026-06-21: **ROSTER HISTORY-ACCURACY HARDENING** — all 9 shipped battles audited; 15 HIGH wrong-fact corrections under "Verified" stamps (the EA-Sports-roster groundwork)

**Newest:** Aaron asked whether the full Chattanooga-grade rigor had ever been applied to the battles already built. It had not — the 7 pre-Opus battles (the Codex/early-built Eastern marquee: Malvern Hill, Antietam, Fredericksburg, Chancellorsville, Gettysburg, plus Shiloh and Bull Run) never got the adversarial history pass; only Vicksburg and Chickamauga had. So a fleet of nine Opus historians audited every named officer in every battle, plus a new consolidated casualty-direction diagnostic (`tools/sweep-all-battles.mjs`). It found **15 high-severity wrong facts shipped under "Verified" stamps** — almost all the same **rank-at-the-battle** class the earlier bug-hunts kept catching (a promotion dated after the battle, applied early). Every one is now fixed and annotated in the data: Malvern Hill (Hunt was a **Colonel**, Jackson a **Major** general — there was no Confederate lieutenant-general grade yet, Porter a **Brigadier**, Hooker a **Major** general); Chancellorsville (Rodes a **Brigadier**, Berry in the **III** Corps, the Stonewall Brigade in **Colston's** division, and a **dead officer** — Hazzard, killed in 1862 — replaced with Capt. Best); Shiloh (Breckinridge a **Brigadier**, Bragg on the **right** wing, Wallace died **April 10**); Gettysburg (Doubleday and Early both **Major** generals, Stannard in the **I** Corps); Vicksburg (Martin L. Smith a **Brigadier** — his major-generalcy came four months after the surrender). Plus a dozen smaller fixes. Two genuinely-disputed cases (Bull Run's Heintzelman, Antietam's Mansfield) were **surfaced, not silently flipped**.

**The principle Aaron locked while we worked:** the game's *outcomes* come from **skill, weapon type, terrain, and tactics** through the one universal combat model — history enters only as the **accurate default equipment** (true soldier counts, gun counts, weapons, terrain), so the *probable* result lands near the real one because the inputs are true, **never because a probe forces it**. We dropped a planned "make the Confederates bleed more at Antietam" balance refight as exactly the kind of over-interpretation to avoid. The casualty checks we keep only validate that the *systems* work (cover shelters, rifles out-range smoothbores, more guns means more fire) so a player can read the field and forecast the odds. None of the corrections changed a single outcome — they're labels and notes (plus one accurate gun-count), and every battle's balance held.

**And a new pillar is on the books:** **"The Soldier's Story"** — an EA-Sports-style roster of *every identifiable individual* (bio, stats, history, portrait, and their army/corps/division/brigade/regiment) and a **play-as-anyone** mode where you pick a real person, choose when and where to start, and carry their actual rank and position through the whole war. It is **scheduled after the Chattanooga + USCT + strategic-arc work**, unified with the loot/survival journey mode, with the interpersonal/romance threads designed as one pillar. Today's rank-and-position hardening is literally its first roster data.

**Verified:** build GATE OK; all nine battle probes green with their balance assertions intact (outcomes byte-preserved), roster 8/8, custom-builder 9/9, the broad tactical-systems suite and boot/t1 green, Classic still paints (`nonBlank:346`), 0 pageerrors.

**Committed in a resumed session** (the prior one died on a 529 API error mid-D92, work uncommitted). Before the commit, an independent adversarial re-verification — an Opus historian per battle plus a critic — re-checked every correction to catch a "fix" that was itself wrong, and caught four more, all fixed: Bull Run's Elzey gloss (his own regiment was the 1st Maryland, not the Virginia regiments); Shiloh's Jacob Ammen, labeled a brigadier but a colonel at the battle (his star came that July); Antietam's Battery B, tagged rifled but six smoothbore Napoleons (the famous double-canister battery — the antietam probe held, so the outcome is unchanged); and Malvern Hill's Hooker, whom D92 had over-promoted to major general by his date-of-rank when his commission was actually issued after the battle (the Seven Days order of battle lists him a brigadier, exactly as the file already keeps Porter, Couch, and Kearny). **Next: Chattanooga**, the Union reversal.
